<p><b>Kraków</b> (<small>Polish pronunciation:&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Polish" title="Help:IPA/Polish">[ˈkrakuf]</a></span> <span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Pl-Krak%C3%B3w.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/47/Pl-Krak%C3%B3w.ogg" class="internal" title="Pl-Kraków.ogg"><small>listen</small></a></span>&#160;<small class="metadata audiolinkinfo" style="cursor:help;">(<a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help"><span style="cursor:help;">help</span></a>·<a href="/wiki/File:Pl-Krak%C3%B3w.ogg" title="File:Pl-Kraków.ogg"><span style="cursor:help;">info</span></a>)</small></span>), also <b>Cracow</b> or <b>Krakow</b> (<span class="nowrap"><small><a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">UK</a>:</small> <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/aʊ/: 'ou' in 'mouth'">aʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <span class="nowrap"><small><a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">US</a>:</small> <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/aʊ/: 'ou' in 'mouth'">aʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span>),<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> is the second largest and <a href="/wiki/List_of_oldest_continuously_inhabited_cities" title="List of oldest continuously inhabited cities">one of the oldest cities</a> in <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>. Situated on the <a href="/wiki/Vistula" title="Vistula">Vistula River</a> (<a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a>: <span lang="pl"><i>Wisła</i></span>) in the <a href="/wiki/Lesser_Poland" title="Lesser Poland">Lesser Poland</a> (<a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a>: <span lang="pl"><i>Małopolska</i></span>) region, the city dates back to the 7th century.<sup id="cite_ref-History_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History-4">[4]</a></sup> Kraków has traditionally been one of the leading centres of Polish academic, cultural, and artistic life and is one of Poland's most important economic hubs. It was the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_the_Kingdom_of_Poland" title="Crown of the Kingdom of Poland">Crown of the Kingdom of Poland</a> from 1038 to 1569; the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a> from 1569 to 1795;<sup id="cite_ref-warsaw-capital-1596_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-warsaw-capital-1596-5">[5]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Free_City_of_Krak%C3%B3w" class="mw-redirect" title="Free City of Kraków">Free City of Kraków</a> from 1815 to 1846; the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Cracow" title="Grand Duchy of Cracow">Grand Duchy of Cracow</a> from 1846 to 1918; and <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w_Voivodeship" title="Kraków Voivodeship">Kraków Voivodeship</a> from the 14th century to 1998. It has been the capital of <a href="/wiki/Lesser_Poland_Voivodeship" title="Lesser Poland Voivodeship">Lesser Poland Voivodeship</a> since 1999.</p>
<p>The city has grown from a <a href="/wiki/Stone_Age" title="Stone Age">Stone Age</a> settlement to Poland's second most important city. It began as a hamlet on <a href="/wiki/Wawel_Castle" title="Wawel Castle">Wawel Hill</a> and was already being reported as a busy trading centre of <a href="/wiki/Slavs" title="Slavs">Slavonic</a> Europe in 965.<sup id="cite_ref-History_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History-4">[4]</a></sup> With the establishment of new universities and cultural venues at the emergence of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Polish_Republic" title="Second Polish Republic">Second Polish Republic</a> in 1918 and throughout the 20th century, Kraków reaffirmed its role as a major national academic and artistic centre. The city has a population of approximately 760,000, with approximately 8&#160;million additional people living within a 100&#160;km (62&#160;mi) radius of its <a href="/wiki/Main_Market_Square,_Krak%C3%B3w" class="mw-redirect" title="Main Market Square, Kraków">main square</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-welcome_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-welcome-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Poland" title="Invasion of Poland">invasion of Poland</a> at the start of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Kraków became the capital of Germany's <a href="/wiki/General_Government" title="General Government">General Government</a>. The Jewish population of the city was forced into a walled zone known as the <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w_Ghetto" title="Kraków Ghetto">Kraków Ghetto</a>, from which they were sent to German <a href="/wiki/Extermination_camp" title="Extermination camp">extermination camps</a> such as the nearby <a href="/wiki/Auschwitz_concentration_camp" title="Auschwitz concentration camp">Auschwitz</a> never to return, and the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_concentration_camps" title="Nazi concentration camps">Nazi concentration camps</a> like <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w-P%C5%82asz%C3%B3w_concentration_camp" title="Kraków-Płaszów concentration camp">Płaszów</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ARC_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ARC-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1978, Karol Wojtyła, <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Krak%C3%B3w" title="Archbishop of Kraków">archbishop of Kraków</a>, was elevated to the <a href="/wiki/Papacy" class="mw-redirect" title="Papacy">papacy</a> as <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_Paul_II" title="Pope John Paul II">Pope John Paul&#160;II</a> — the first <a href="/wiki/Slavic_peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Slavic peoples">Slavic</a> pope ever, and the first non-Italian pope in 455 years.<sup id="cite_ref-Clark_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-8">[8]</a></sup> Also that year, <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> approved the first ever sites for its new <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage List</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w_Old_Town" title="Kraków Old Town">the entire Old Town</a> in inscribing <i>Cracow's Historic Centre</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-unesco-properties-pl_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unesco-properties-pl-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-unesco-02com_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unesco-02com-10">[10]</a></sup> Kraków is classified as a global city with the ranking of high sufficiency by <a href="/wiki/GaWC" class="mw-redirect" title="GaWC">GaWC</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Cited as one of Europe's most beautiful cities,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> as well as one of the most unique destinations in the world,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> its extensive cultural heritage across the epochs of <a href="/wiki/Gothic_architecture" title="Gothic architecture">Gothic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_architecture" title="Renaissance architecture">Renaissance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baroque_architecture" title="Baroque architecture">Baroque architecture</a> includes the <a href="/wiki/Wawel_Cathedral" title="Wawel Cathedral">Wawel Cathedral</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Wawel_castle" class="mw-redirect" title="Wawel castle">Royal Castle</a> on the banks of the <a href="/wiki/Vistula_river" class="mw-redirect" title="Vistula river">Vistula river</a>, the <a href="/wiki/St._Mary%27s_Basilica,_Krak%C3%B3w" title="St. Mary's Basilica, Kraków">St. Mary's Basilica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saints_Peter_and_Paul_Church,_Krak%C3%B3w" title="Saints Peter and Paul Church, Kraków">Saints Peter and Paul Church</a> and the largest <a href="/wiki/Medieval" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval">medieval</a> market square in Europe, the <a href="/wiki/Main_Square,_Krak%C3%B3w" title="Main Square, Kraków">Rynek Główny</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Kraków is home to <a href="/wiki/Jagiellonian_University" title="Jagiellonian University">Jagiellonian University</a>, one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_oldest_universities_in_continuous_operation" title="List of oldest universities in continuous operation">oldest universities in the world</a> and traditionally Poland's most reputable institution of higher learning.</p>
<p>In 2000, Kraków was named <a href="/wiki/European_Capital_of_Culture" title="European Capital of Culture">European Capital of Culture</a>. In 2013 Kraków was officially approved as a <a href="/wiki/UNESCO_City_of_Literature" class="mw-redirect" title="UNESCO City of Literature">UNESCO City of Literature</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The city hosted the <a href="/wiki/World_Youth_Day" title="World Youth Day">World Youth Day</a> in July 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Krak%C3%B3w&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The name of Kraków is traditionally derived from <a href="/wiki/Krakus" title="Krakus">Krakus</a> (Krak, Grakch), the legendary founder of Kraków and a ruler of the tribe of <a href="/wiki/Lechites" title="Lechites">Lechitians</a>. In Polish, <span lang="pl"><i>Kraków</i></span> is an archaic <a href="/wiki/Possessive_case" class="mw-redirect" title="Possessive case">possessive</a> form of <i>Krak</i> and essentially means "Krak's (town)". Krakus's name may derive from "krakula", a <a href="/wiki/Proto-Slavic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Slavic language">Proto-Slavic</a> word<sup id="cite_ref-Krak_or_Krakus.3F_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krak_or_Krakus.3F-17">[17]</a></sup> meaning a judge's staff, or a Proto-Slavic word "krak" meaning an <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oak</a>, once a <a href="/wiki/Sacred_tree" class="mw-redirect" title="Sacred tree">sacred tree</a> most often associated with the concept of <a href="/wiki/Genealogy" title="Genealogy">genealogy</a>. The first mention of Prince Krakus (then written as <i>Grakch</i>) dates back to 1190, although the town existed as early as the 7th century, inhabited by the tribe of <a href="/wiki/Vistulans" title="Vistulans">Vistulans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-History_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>The city's full official name is <span lang="pl"><i>Stołeczne Królewskie Miasto Kraków</i></span>,<sup id="cite_ref-bip.krakow-UCHWA.C5.81A_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bip.krakow-UCHWA.C5.81A-18">[18]</a></sup> which can be translated as "<a href="/wiki/Royal_city" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal city">Royal Capital City</a> of Kraków". In English, a person born or living in Kraków is a Cracovian (<a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a>: <span lang="pl"><i>krakowianin</i></span>). The most widespread modern English version of the city's name is Krakow, while Cracow was used in 90s.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Krak%C3%B3w&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Krak%C3%B3w" title="History of Kraków">History of Kraków</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Krak%C3%B3w" title="Timeline of Kraków">Timeline of Kraków</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Krakow_nagrobek_Kazimierza_W.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Krakow_nagrobek_Kazimierza_W.jpg/170px-Krakow_nagrobek_Kazimierza_W.jpg" width="170" height="296" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Krakow_nagrobek_Kazimierza_W.jpg/255px-Krakow_nagrobek_Kazimierza_W.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Krakow_nagrobek_Kazimierza_W.jpg/340px-Krakow_nagrobek_Kazimierza_W.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1844" data-file-height="3216" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Krakow_nagrobek_Kazimierza_W.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Royal tomb of king <a href="/wiki/Casimir_III_the_Great" title="Casimir III the Great">Casimir III the Great</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Wawel_Cathedral" title="Wawel Cathedral">Wawel Cathedral</a>—Kraków was the capital of Poland from 1038 to 1596</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Kraków's <a href="/wiki/Prehistory" title="Prehistory">early history</a> begins with evidence of a Stone Age settlement on the present site of the Wawel Hill.<sup id="cite_ref-Wawel_Krak.C3.B3w_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wawel_Krak.C3.B3w-20">[20]</a></sup> A legend attributes Kraków's founding to the mythical ruler <a href="/wiki/Krakus" title="Krakus">Krakus</a>, who built it above a cave occupied by a <a href="/wiki/Dragon" title="Dragon">dragon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smok_Wawelski" class="mw-redirect" title="Smok Wawelski">Smok Wawelski</a>. The first written record of the city's name dates back to 965, when Kraków was described as a notable commercial centre controlled first by Moravia (876–879), but captured by a Bohemian duke <a href="/wiki/Boleslaus_I_of_Bohemia" class="mw-redirect" title="Boleslaus I of Bohemia">Boleslaus I</a> in 955.<sup id="cite_ref-krakow.pl-2_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-krakow.pl-2-21">[21]</a></sup> The first acclaimed ruler of Poland, <a href="/wiki/Mieszko_I_of_Poland" title="Mieszko I of Poland">Mieszko I</a>, took Kraków from the Bohemians and incorporated it into the holdings of the <a href="/wiki/Piast_dynasty" title="Piast dynasty">Piast dynasty</a> towards the end of his reign.<sup id="cite_ref-Cawley_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cawley-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1038, Kraków became the seat of the Polish government.<sup id="cite_ref-History_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History-4">[4]</a></sup> By the end of the 10th century, the city was a leading centre of trade.<sup id="cite_ref-Van_Dongen_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Van_Dongen-23">[23]</a></sup> Brick buildings were constructed, including the Royal <a href="/wiki/Wawel_Castle" title="Wawel Castle">Wawel Castle</a> with St. Felix and Adaukt Rotunda, <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque</a> churches such as <a href="/wiki/Church_of_St._Adalbert,_Krak%C3%B3w" title="Church of St. Adalbert, Kraków">St. Adalbert's</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wawel_Cathedral" title="Wawel Cathedral">a cathedral</a>, and <a href="/wiki/St._Mary%27s_Basilica,_Krak%C3%B3w" title="St. Mary's Basilica, Kraków">a basilica</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rosik_-_Urba.C5.84czyk_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rosik_-_Urba.C5.84czyk-24">[24]</a></sup> The city was almost entirely destroyed during the <a href="/wiki/First_Mongol_invasion_of_Poland" title="First Mongol invasion of Poland">Mongol invasion</a> of 1241. It was rebuilt practically identical,<sup id="cite_ref-Akt_lokacyjny_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Akt_lokacyjny-25">[25]</a></sup> based on new location act and <a href="/wiki/Municipal_corporation" title="Municipal corporation">incorporated</a> in 1257 by the high duke <a href="/wiki/Boles%C5%82aw_V_the_Chaste" title="Bolesław V the Chaste">Bolesław V the Chaste</a> who following the example of <a href="/wiki/Wroc%C5%82aw" title="Wrocław">Wrocław</a>, introduced city rights modelled on the <a href="/wiki/Magdeburg_rights" title="Magdeburg rights">Magdeburg law</a> allowing for tax benefits and new trade privileges for the citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-Strzala2_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strzala2-26">[26]</a></sup> In 1259, the city was again ravaged by the Mongols. A third attack in 1287 was repelled thanks in part to the <a href="/wiki/St._Florian%27s_Gate" title="St. Florian's Gate">new built fortifications</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kolodziejczyk_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kolodziejczyk-27">[27]</a></sup> In 1335, King <a href="/wiki/Casimir_III_of_Poland" class="mw-redirect" title="Casimir III of Poland">Casimir III of Poland</a> (Kazimierz in Polish) declared the two western suburbs to be a new city named after him, <a href="/wiki/Kazimierz" title="Kazimierz">Kazimierz</a> (<i>Casimiria</i> in Latin). The defensive walls were erected around the central section of Kazimierz in 1362, and a plot was set aside for the <a href="/wiki/Augustinian" class="mw-redirect" title="Augustinian">Augustinian</a> order next to <a href="/wiki/Ska%C5%82ka" title="Skałka">Skałka</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-.C5.9Awiszczowski_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-.C5.9Awiszczowski-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>The city rose to prominence in 1364, when Casimir III of Poland founded the <a href="/wiki/Jagiellonian_University" title="Jagiellonian University">University of Kraków</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-The_establishment_of_a_university_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_establishment_of_a_university-29">[29]</a></sup> the second oldest university in central Europe after the Charles University in Prague. King Casimir also began work on a campus for the Academy in Kazimierz, but he died in 1370 and the campus was never completed. The city continued to grow under the joint <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Lithuania" title="Grand Duchy of Lithuania">Lithuanian</a>-Polish <a href="/wiki/Jagiellon_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Jagiellon dynasty">Jagiellon dynasty</a>. As the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Poland_(1385%E2%80%931569)" title="Kingdom of Poland (1385–1569)">Kingdom of Poland</a> and a member of the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a>, the city attracted many craftsmen, businesses, and <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">guilds</a> as science and the arts began to flourish.<sup id="cite_ref-poloniahans_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-poloniahans-30">[30]</a></sup> The royal chancery and the University ensured a first flourishing of Polish literary culture in the city.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Kraków's_&quot;Golden_Age&quot;"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Krak.C3.B3w.27s_.22Golden_Age.22">Kraków's "Golden Age"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Krak%C3%B3w&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Kraków's &quot;Golden Age&quot;">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The 15th and 16th centuries were known as Poland's <i>Złoty Wiek</i> or <a href="/wiki/Golden_Age" title="Golden Age">Golden Age</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NormanDavies_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NormanDavies-32">[32]</a></sup> Many works of <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_in_Poland" title="Renaissance in Poland">Polish Renaissance</a> art and architecture were created,<sup id="cite_ref-Mikos_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikos-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-unescoancient_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unescoancient-34">[34]</a></sup> including ancient synagogues in Kraków's Jewish quarter located in the north-eastern part of Kazimierz, such as the <a href="/wiki/Old_Synagogue_(Krak%C3%B3w)" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Synagogue (Kraków)">Old Synagogue</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-infosyn_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-infosyn-35">[35]</a></sup> During the reign of <a href="/wiki/Casimir_IV_Jagiellon" title="Casimir IV Jagiellon">Casimir IV</a>, various artists came to work and live in Kraków, and <a href="/wiki/Johann_Haller" title="Johann Haller">Johann Haller</a> established a <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> in the city<sup id="cite_ref-Haller_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haller-36">[36]</a></sup> after <a href="/wiki/Kasper_Straube" title="Kasper Straube">Kasper Straube</a> had printed the <a href="/wiki/Almanach_cracoviense_ad_annum_1474" title="Almanach cracoviense ad annum 1474">Calendarium Cracoviense</a>, the first work printed in Poland, in 1473.<sup id="cite_ref-Norman_Davies.2C_God.27s_Playground.2C_vol.1.2C_chapter_5_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norman_Davies.2C_God.27s_Playground.2C_vol.1.2C_chapter_5-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wieslaw_Wydra_88_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wieslaw_Wydra_88-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a href="/wiki/File:View_of_Krak%C3%B3w_near_the_end_of_the_16th_century.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/View_of_Krak%C3%B3w_near_the_end_of_the_16th_century.jpg/360px-View_of_Krak%C3%B3w_near_the_end_of_the_16th_century.jpg" width="360" height="117" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/View_of_Krak%C3%B3w_near_the_end_of_the_16th_century.jpg/540px-View_of_Krak%C3%B3w_near_the_end_of_the_16th_century.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/View_of_Krak%C3%B3w_near_the_end_of_the_16th_century.jpg/720px-View_of_Krak%C3%B3w_near_the_end_of_the_16th_century.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4539" data-file-height="1480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:View_of_Krak%C3%B3w_near_the_end_of_the_16th_century.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
View of Kraków (<i>Cracovia</i>) near the end of the 16th-century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1495, King <a href="/wiki/John_I_Albert" title="John I Albert">John I Albert</a> expelled the Jews from the city walls of Krakow; they moved to <a href="/wiki/Kazimierz" title="Kazimierz">Kazimierz</a> (now a district of Krakow).<sup id="cite_ref-findingyourjewishroots4_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-findingyourjewishroots4-39">[39]</a></sup> However, they were still allowed to trade on the <a href="/wiki/Main_Square,_Krak%C3%B3w" title="Main Square, Kraków">Main Square</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-findingyourjewishroots4_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-findingyourjewishroots4-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1520, the most famous <a href="/wiki/Church_bell" title="Church bell">church bell</a> in Poland, named <i><a href="/wiki/Zygmunt_(bell)" class="mw-redirect" title="Zygmunt (bell)">Zygmunt</a></i> after <a href="/wiki/Sigismund_I_the_Old" title="Sigismund I the Old">Sigismund I of Poland</a>, was cast by Hans Behem.<sup id="cite_ref-dzwon_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dzwon-40">[40]</a></sup> At that time, <a href="/wiki/Hans_D%C3%BCrer" title="Hans Dürer">Hans Dürer</a>, a younger brother of artist and thinker <a href="/wiki/Albrecht_D%C3%BCrer" title="Albrecht Dürer">Albrecht Dürer</a>, was Sigismund's <a href="/wiki/Court_painter" title="Court painter">court painter</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HansDur_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HansDur-41">[41]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hans_von_Kulmbach" title="Hans von Kulmbach">Hans von Kulmbach</a> made <a href="/wiki/Altarpiece" title="Altarpiece">altarpieces</a> for several churches.<sup id="cite_ref-Kulmbach_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kulmbach-42">[42]</a></sup> In 1553, the Kazimierz district council gave the Jewish <a href="/wiki/Kehilla_(modern)" title="Kehilla (modern)">Qahal</a> a licence for the right to build their own interior walls across the western section of the already existing defensive walls. The walls were expanded again in 1608 due to the growth of the community and influx of Jews from Bohemia.<sup id="cite_ref-Kazimierz.com_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kazimierz.com-43">[43]</a></sup> In 1572, King <a href="/wiki/Sigismund_II_Augustus" title="Sigismund II Augustus">Sigismund II</a>, the last of the Jagiellons, died childless. The Polish throne passed to <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_France" title="Henry III of France">Henry III of France</a> and then to other foreign-based rulers in rapid succession, causing a decline in the city's importance that was worsened by pillaging during the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Swedish_wars" title="Polish–Swedish wars">Swedish invasion</a> and by an outbreak of <a href="/wiki/Bubonic_plague" title="Bubonic plague">bubonic plague</a> that left 20,000 of the city's residents dead. In 1596, <a href="/wiki/Sigismund_III_Vasa" title="Sigismund III Vasa">Sigismund III</a> of the Swedish <a href="/wiki/House_of_Vasa" title="House of Vasa">House of Vasa</a> moved the administrative capital of the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a> from Kraków to <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-warsaw-capital-1596_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-warsaw-capital-1596-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Krak%C3%B3w&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:J%C3%B3zef_Brodowski_002-1(crop).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/J%C3%B3zef_Brodowski_002-1%28crop%29.JPG/220px-J%C3%B3zef_Brodowski_002-1%28crop%29.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/J%C3%B3zef_Brodowski_002-1%28crop%29.JPG/330px-J%C3%B3zef_Brodowski_002-1%28crop%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/J%C3%B3zef_Brodowski_002-1%28crop%29.JPG/440px-J%C3%B3zef_Brodowski_002-1%28crop%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2920" data-file-height="2192" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:J%C3%B3zef_Brodowski_002-1(crop).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Act of granting the constitution to the <a href="/wiki/Free_City_of_Cracow" title="Free City of Cracow">Free City of Cracow</a>—Kraków was independent city republic between 1815 and 1846</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Already weakened during the 18th century, by the mid-1790s the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a> had twice been <a href="/wiki/Partitions_of_Poland" title="Partitions of Poland">partitioned</a> by its neighbors: <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Monarchy" title="Habsburg Monarchy">Habsburg empire</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Prussia" title="Kingdom of Prussia">Prussia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Polish_struggle_for_freedom_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Polish_struggle_for_freedom-44">[44]</a></sup> In 1791, the Austrian Emperor <a href="/wiki/Joseph_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Joseph II, Holy Roman Emperor">Joseph II</a> changed the status of Kazimierz as a separate city and made it into a district of Kraków. The richer Jewish families began to move out. However, because of the injunction against travel on the <a href="/wiki/Shabbat" title="Shabbat">Sabbath</a>, most Jewish families stayed relatively close to the historic synagogues. In 1794, <a href="/wiki/Tadeusz_Ko%C5%9Bciuszko" title="Tadeusz Kościuszko">Tadeusz Kościuszko</a> initiated an unsuccessful <a href="/wiki/Ko%C5%9Bciuszko_Uprising" title="Kościuszko Uprising">insurrection</a> in <a href="/wiki/Main_Square,_Krak%C3%B3w" title="Main Square, Kraków">the town's Main Square</a> which, in spite of his victorious <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Rac%C5%82awice" title="Battle of Racławice">Battle of Racławice</a> against a numerically superior <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Russian_Army" title="Imperial Russian Army">Russian army</a>, resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Third_Partition_of_Poland" title="Third Partition of Poland">third and final partition of Poland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GR_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GR-45">[45]</a></sup> In 1809, <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon Bonaparte</a> captured former Polish territories from Austria and made the town part of the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Warsaw" title="Duchy of Warsaw">Duchy of Warsaw</a>. Following Napoleon's defeat, the 1815 <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Vienna" title="Congress of Vienna">Congress of Vienna</a> restored the pre-war boundaries but also created the partially independent <a href="/wiki/Free_City_of_Krak%C3%B3w" class="mw-redirect" title="Free City of Kraków">Free City of Kraków</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Republic_of_Cracow_.28historical_state.2C_Poland.29_.E2.80.93_Britannica_Online_Encyclopedia_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Republic_of_Cracow_.28historical_state.2C_Poland.29_.E2.80.93_Britannica_Online_Encyclopedia-46">[46]</a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w_Uprising" class="mw-redirect" title="Kraków Uprising">insurrection in 1846</a> failed,<sup id="cite_ref-Frommer_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frommer-47">[47]</a></sup> resulting in the city being annexed by Austria under the name the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Cracow" title="Grand Duchy of Cracow">Grand Duchy of Cracow</a> (<a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a>: <span lang="pl"><i>Wielkie Księstwo Krakowskie</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <span lang="de"><i>Großherzogtum Krakau</i></span>).<sup id="cite_ref-Chambers.27s_encyclopaedia:_a..._-_Google_Books_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chambers.27s_encyclopaedia:_a..._-_Google_Books-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1866, Austria granted a degree of autonomy to <a href="/wiki/Galicia_(Eastern_Europe)" title="Galicia (Eastern Europe)">Galicia</a> after its own defeat in the <a href="/wiki/Austro-Prussian_War" title="Austro-Prussian War">Austro-Prussian War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-.28.27.27see:_Franz_Joseph_I_granted_Krak.C3.B3w_the_municipal_government.27.27.29_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-.28.27.27see:_Franz_Joseph_I_granted_Krak.C3.B3w_the_municipal_government.27.27.29-49">[49]</a></sup> Politically freer Kraków became a Polish national symbol and a centre of culture and art, known frequently as the "Polish Athens" (<i><span lang="pl">Polskie Ateny</span></i>) or "Polish Mecca".<sup id="cite_ref-Szara_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Szara-50">[50]</a></sup> Many leading Polish artists of the period resided in Kraków,<sup id="cite_ref-google_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google-51">[51]</a></sup> among them the seminal painter <a href="/wiki/Jan_Matejko" title="Jan Matejko">Jan Matejko</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Matejko_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matejko-52">[52]</a></sup> laid to rest at <a href="/wiki/Rakowicki_Cemetery" title="Rakowicki Cemetery">Rakowicki Cemetery</a>, and the founder of modern Polish drama, <a href="/wiki/Stanis%C5%82aw_Wyspia%C5%84ski" title="Stanisław Wyspiański">Stanisław Wyspiański</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-culture_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-culture-53">[53]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fin_de_si%C3%A8cle" title="Fin de siècle">Fin de siècle</a> Kraków evolved into a modern metropolis; <a href="/wiki/Tap_water" title="Tap water">running water</a> and electric <a href="/wiki/Tram" title="Tram">streetcars</a> were introduced in 1901, and between 1910 and 1915, Kraków and its surrounding suburban communities were gradually combined into a single administrative unit called Greater Kraków (<i><span lang="pl">Wielki Kraków</span></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Becoming_Metropolitan:_Urban_Selfhood_and_the_Making_of_Modern_Cracow_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Becoming_Metropolitan:_Urban_Selfhood_and_the_Making_of_Modern_Cracow-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kalendarium_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kalendarium-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the outbreak of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> on 3 August 1914, <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3zef_Pi%C5%82sudski" title="Józef Piłsudski">Józef Piłsudski</a> formed a small <a href="/wiki/Cadre_(military)" title="Cadre (military)">cadre</a> <a href="/wiki/Military_organization" title="Military organization">military unit</a>, the <a href="/wiki/First_Cadre_Company" title="First Cadre Company">First Cadre Company</a>—the predecessor of the <a href="/wiki/Polish_Legions_in_World_War_I" title="Polish Legions in World War I">Polish Legions</a>—which set out from Kraków to fight for the liberation of Poland.<sup id="cite_ref-Urb_171-172_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urb_171-172-56">[56]</a></sup> The city was briefly besieged by Russian troops in November 1914.<sup id="cite_ref-twierdza_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twierdza-57">[57]</a></sup> Austrian rule in Kraków ended in 1918 when the <a href="/wiki/Polish_Liquidation_Committee" title="Polish Liquidation Committee">Polish Liquidation Committee</a> assumed power.<sup id="cite_ref-Eastern_Europe:_an_introduction_to..._-_Google_Books_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eastern_Europe:_an_introduction_to..._-_Google_Books-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopedia_of_Rusyn_history_and..._-_Google_Books_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopedia_of_Rusyn_history_and..._-_Google_Books-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century_to_the_present">20th century to the present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Krak%C3%B3w&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 20th century to the present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rozw%C3%B3j_terytorialny_Krakowa_-_EN.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Rozw%C3%B3j_terytorialny_Krakowa_-_EN.svg/300px-Rozw%C3%B3j_terytorialny_Krakowa_-_EN.svg.png" width="300" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Rozw%C3%B3j_terytorialny_Krakowa_-_EN.svg/450px-Rozw%C3%B3j_terytorialny_Krakowa_-_EN.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Rozw%C3%B3j_terytorialny_Krakowa_-_EN.svg/600px-Rozw%C3%B3j_terytorialny_Krakowa_-_EN.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="804" data-file-height="466" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rozw%C3%B3j_terytorialny_Krakowa_-_EN.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Kraków's territorial growth from the late 18th- to the 20th-century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>With the emergence of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Polish_Republic" title="Second Polish Republic">Second Polish Republic</a>, Kraków resumed its role as a major academic and cultural centre, with the establishment of new universities such as the <a href="/wiki/AGH_University_of_Science_and_Technology" title="AGH University of Science and Technology">AGH University of Science and Technology</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Jan_Matejko_Academy_of_Fine_Arts" title="Jan Matejko Academy of Fine Arts">Jan Matejko Academy of Fine Arts</a>, including a number of new and essential vocational schools. It became an important cultural centre for the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Poland" title="History of the Jews in Poland">Polish Jews</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Zionism" title="Zionism">Zionist</a> <a href="/wiki/Youth_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Youth movement">youth movement</a> relatively strong among the city's Jewish population.<sup id="cite_ref-Krakow_old_scenes.2C_including_historical_photographs_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krakow_old_scenes.2C_including_historical_photographs-60">[60]</a></sup> Kraków was also an influential centre of Jewish spiritual life, with all its manifestations of religious observance from <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Judaism" title="Orthodox Judaism">Orthodox</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Hasidic_Judaism" title="Hasidic Judaism">Chasidic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reform_Judaism" title="Reform Judaism">Reform</a> flourishing side by side.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Krakow-Ghetto-checkpoint.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e6/Krakow-Ghetto-checkpoint.jpg/220px-Krakow-Ghetto-checkpoint.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e6/Krakow-Ghetto-checkpoint.jpg/330px-Krakow-Ghetto-checkpoint.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e6/Krakow-Ghetto-checkpoint.jpg/440px-Krakow-Ghetto-checkpoint.jpg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="313" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Krakow-Ghetto-checkpoint.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w_Ghetto" title="Kraków Ghetto">Kraków Ghetto</a>, 1942—a German checkpoint during operation <i><span lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Operation_Reinhard_in_Krak%C3%B3w" title="Operation Reinhard in Kraków">Aktion Krakau</a></span></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following the invasion of Poland by Nazi Germany in September 1939, the city became part of the <a href="/wiki/General_Government" title="General Government">General Government</a>, a separate administrative region of the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Third Reich</a>, and from 4 November 1939; it became the capital of this administration. The General Government was ruled by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Frank" title="Hans Frank">Hans Frank</a> who was based in the city's <a href="/wiki/Wawel_Castle" title="Wawel Castle">Wawel Castle</a>. The Nazis envisioned turning Kraków into a completely Germanised city; after removal of all the Jews and Poles, renaming of locations and streets into the German language, and sponsorship of propaganda trying to portray it as a historically German city.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> During an operation called "<span lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Sonderaktion_Krakau" title="Sonderaktion Krakau">Sonderaktion Krakau</a></span>", more than 180 university professors and academics were arrested and sent to <a href="/wiki/Sachsenhausen_concentration_camp" title="Sachsenhausen concentration camp">Sachsenhausen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dachau_concentration_camp" title="Dachau concentration camp">Dachau</a> <a href="/wiki/Nazi_concentration_camps" title="Nazi concentration camps">concentration camps</a>, though the survivors were later released on the request of prominent Italians.<sup id="cite_ref-16B._Eastern_Europe_in_World_War_II:_October_1939_.E2.80.93_May_1945._62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16B._Eastern_Europe_in_World_War_II:_October_1939_.E2.80.93_May_1945.-62">[62]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sonderaktion_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sonderaktion-63">[63]</a></sup> The Jewish population was first <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w_Ghetto" title="Kraków Ghetto">confined to a ghetto</a> in which many died of illness or starvation. Those in the ghetto were later murdered or <a href="/wiki/Operation_Reinhard_in_Krak%C3%B3w" title="Operation Reinhard in Kraków">sent to concentration camps</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w-P%C5%82asz%C3%B3w_concentration_camp" title="Kraków-Płaszów concentration camp">Płaszów</a> and <a href="/wiki/Auschwitz_concentration_camp" title="Auschwitz concentration camp">Auschwitz</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Krak.C3.B3w_Ghetto_1940.E2.80.931943_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Krak.C3.B3w_Ghetto_1940.E2.80.931943-64">[64]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Roman_Polanski" title="Roman Polanski">Roman Polanski</a>, the film director, is a survivor of the Kraków Ghetto, while <a href="/wiki/Oskar_Schindler" title="Oskar Schindler">Oskar Schindler</a> selected employees from the ghetto to work in his <a href="/wiki/Vitreous_enamel" title="Vitreous enamel">enamelware</a> plant, <i><span lang="de">Deutsche Emailwaren Fabrik</span></i> (<i><span lang="de">Emalia</span></i> for short) saving them from the camps.<sup id="cite_ref-All_for_Love_-_Google_Books_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-All_for_Love_-_Google_Books-65">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Oskar_Schindler:_The_Untold_Account..._-_Google_Books_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oskar_Schindler:_The_Untold_Account..._-_Google_Books-66">[66]</a></sup> Although <a href="/wiki/World_War_II_looting_of_Poland" title="World War II looting of Poland">looted by occupational authorities</a>, Kraków remained relatively undamaged at the end of World War II,<sup id="cite_ref-LukZaw_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LukZaw-67">[67]</a></sup> sparing most of the city's historical and architectural legacy. Soviet forces entered the city on 18 January 1945, and began arresting Poles loyal to the <a href="/wiki/Polish_government-in-exile" title="Polish government-in-exile">Polish government-in-exile</a> or those who had served in the <a href="/wiki/Home_Army" title="Home Army">Home Army</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the war, under the <a href="/wiki/Polish_People%27s_Republic" title="Polish People's Republic">Polish People's Republic</a>, the intellectual and academic community of Kraków was put under complete political control. The universities were soon deprived of printing rights and autonomy.<sup id="cite_ref-autonomy_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autonomy-69">[69]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Stalinism" title="Stalinism">Stalinist</a> government ordered the construction of the country's largest <a href="/wiki/Steel_mill" title="Steel mill">steel mill</a> in the newly created suburb of <a href="/wiki/Nowa_Huta" title="Nowa Huta">Nowa Huta</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-krakow_history_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-krakow_history-70">[70]</a></sup> The creation of the giant Lenin Steelworks (now <a href="/wiki/Tadeusz_Sendzimir_Steelworks" title="Tadeusz Sendzimir Steelworks">Sendzimir</a> Steelworks owned by <a href="/wiki/Mittal_Steel_Company" title="Mittal Steel Company">Mittal</a>) sealed Kraków's transformation from a university city, into an industrial centre.<sup id="cite_ref-communist_era_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-communist_era-71">[71]</a></sup> The new working-class, drawn by the industrialisation of Kraków, contributed to rapid population growth.</p>
<p>In an effort that spanned two decades, <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_Paul_II" title="Pope John Paul II">Karol Wojtyła</a>, cardinal archbishop of Kraków, successfully lobbied for permission to build the first churches in the newly industrial suburbs.<sup id="cite_ref-communist_era_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-communist_era-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NH-anthology_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NH-anthology-72">[72]</a></sup> In 1978, Wojtyła was elevated to the <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">papacy</a> as <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_Paul_II" title="Pope John Paul II">John Paul II</a>, the first non-Italian pope in 455 years. In the same year, <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> placed Kraków Old Town on the first-ever list of <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Sites</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Krak%C3%B3w&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zakrz%C3%B3wek_(Krak%C3%B3w)-kamieniolom.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Zakrz%C3%B3wek_%28Krak%C3%B3w%29-kamieniolom.jpg/260px-Zakrz%C3%B3wek_%28Krak%C3%B3w%29-kamieniolom.jpg" width="260" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Zakrz%C3%B3wek_%28Krak%C3%B3w%29-kamieniolom.jpg/390px-Zakrz%C3%B3wek_%28Krak%C3%B3w%29-kamieniolom.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Zakrz%C3%B3wek_%28Krak%C3%B3w%29-kamieniolom.jpg/520px-Zakrz%C3%B3wek_%28Krak%C3%B3w%29-kamieniolom.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1228" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zakrz%C3%B3wek_(Krak%C3%B3w)-kamieniolom.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Zalew Zakrzówek</i>, a flooded quarry within the city limits of Kraków</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Kraków lies in the southern part of Poland, on the <a href="/wiki/Vistula_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Vistula River">Vistula River</a>, in a valley at the foot of the <a href="/wiki/Carpathian_Mountains" title="Carpathian Mountains">Carpathian Mountains</a>, 219&#160;m (719&#160;ft) <a href="/wiki/Above_mean_sea_level" class="mw-redirect" title="Above mean sea level">above sea level</a>; halfway between the <a href="/wiki/Polish_Jura_Chain" class="mw-redirect" title="Polish Jura Chain">Jurassic Rock Upland</a> (<a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a>: <span lang="pl"><i>Jura Krakowsko-Częstochowska</i></span>) to the north, and the <a href="/wiki/Tatra_Mountains" title="Tatra Mountains">Tatra Mountains</a> 100&#160;km (62&#160;mi) to the south, constituting the natural border with <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">the Czech Republic</a>; 230&#160;km (143&#160;mi) west from the border with <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>. There are five <a href="/wiki/Nature_reserve" title="Nature reserve">nature reserves</a> in Kraków, with a combined area of ca. 48.6 hectares (120 acres). Due to their ecological value, these areas are legally protected. The western part of the city, along its northern and north-western side, borders an area of international significance known as the Jurassic <a href="/wiki/Bielany,_Krak%C3%B3w" title="Bielany, Kraków">Bielany</a>-<a href="/wiki/Tyniec" title="Tyniec">Tyniec</a> refuge. The main motives for the protection of this area include plant and animal wildlife and the area's <a href="/wiki/Geomorphology" title="Geomorphology">geomorphological</a> features and landscape.<sup id="cite_ref-Pattern_of_karst_landscape_of_the_Cracow_Upland_.28South_Poland.29_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pattern_of_karst_landscape_of_the_Cracow_Upland_.28South_Poland.29-73">[73]</a></sup> Another part of the city is located within the ecological 'corridor' of the Vistula River valley. This corridor is also assessed as being of international significance as part of the Pan-European ecological network.<sup id="cite_ref-The_forms_of_nature_protection_within_the_city_limits_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_forms_of_nature_protection_within_the_city_limits-74">[74]</a></sup> The city centre is situated on the left (northern) bank of the river.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Krak%C3%B3w&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Krakow_Klasztor_Norbertanek.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Krakow_Klasztor_Norbertanek.jpg/220px-Krakow_Klasztor_Norbertanek.jpg" width="220" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Krakow_Klasztor_Norbertanek.jpg/330px-Krakow_Klasztor_Norbertanek.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Krakow_Klasztor_Norbertanek.jpg/440px-Krakow_Klasztor_Norbertanek.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3513" data-file-height="2125" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Krakow_Klasztor_Norbertanek.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Convent of Norbertine Sisters in Kraków-Zwierzyniec and the <a href="/wiki/Vistula_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Vistula River">Vistula River</a> during the Winter season</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Kraków has an <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_climate" title="Oceanic climate">oceanic climate</a> (<i>Cfb</i>) according to the <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen climate classification</a> system, one of the easternmost localities in Europe to do so. A mere 100&#160;km (62&#160;mi) north-east of Kraków (east of <a href="/wiki/Tarn%C3%B3w" title="Tarnów">Tarnów</a>, and north of <a href="/wiki/Kielce" title="Kielce">Kielce</a>), the January mean dips below −3&#160;°C (27&#160;°F) and thus becomes continental (<i>Dfb</i>) in nature. The Kraków climate is also influenced by its far inland position, with significant temperature differences between seasons. Average temperatures in summer range from 17.0 to 19.2&#160;°C (63 to 67&#160;°F) and in winter from −2.0 to −0.6&#160;°C (28 to 31&#160;°F). The average annual temperature is 8.7&#160;°C (48&#160;°F). In summer temperatures often exceed 25&#160;°C (77&#160;°F), and even 30&#160;°C (86&#160;°F), while winter drops to −5&#160;°C (23&#160;°F) at night and about 0&#160;°C (32&#160;°F) at day; during very cold nights the temperature can drop to −15&#160;°C (5&#160;°F). Since Kraków lies near the Tatra Mountains, there are often occurrences of <a href="/wiki/Halny" title="Halny">halny</a> blowing (a <a href="/wiki/Foehn_wind" title="Foehn wind">foehn wind</a>), causing temperatures to rise rapidly, and even in winter reach up to 20&#160;°C (68&#160;°F).</p>
<p>The climate table below presents weather data from the years 2000–2012 although the official Köppen reference period was from 1981–2010. According to ongoing measurements, the temperature has increased during these years as compared with the last series. This increase averages about 0.6&#160;°C over all months. Warming is most pronounced during the winter months, with an increase of more than 1.0&#160;°C in January.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cityscape">Cityscape</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Krak%C3%B3w&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Cityscape">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Developed over many centuries, Kraków provides a showcase setting for many historic styles of architecture. As the city expanded, so too did the architectural achievements of its builders. It is for this reason that the variations in style and urban planning are so easily recognisable.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A-10_Sukiennice_w_Krakowie_Krak%C3%B3w,_Rynek_G%C5%82%C3%B3wny_MM.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/A-10_Sukiennice_w_Krakowie_Krak%C3%B3w%2C_Rynek_G%C5%82%C3%B3wny_MM.jpg/220px-A-10_Sukiennice_w_Krakowie_Krak%C3%B3w%2C_Rynek_G%C5%82%C3%B3wny_MM.jpg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/A-10_Sukiennice_w_Krakowie_Krak%C3%B3w%2C_Rynek_G%C5%82%C3%B3wny_MM.jpg/330px-A-10_Sukiennice_w_Krakowie_Krak%C3%B3w%2C_Rynek_G%C5%82%C3%B3wny_MM.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/A-10_Sukiennice_w_Krakowie_Krak%C3%B3w%2C_Rynek_G%C5%82%C3%B3wny_MM.jpg/440px-A-10_Sukiennice_w_Krakowie_Krak%C3%B3w%2C_Rynek_G%C5%82%C3%B3wny_MM.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2896" data-file-height="1944" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A-10_Sukiennice_w_Krakowie_Krak%C3%B3w,_Rynek_G%C5%82%C3%B3wny_MM.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_in_Poland" title="Renaissance in Poland">Renaissance</a> Cloth Hall (<i><a href="/wiki/Sukiennice" class="mw-redirect" title="Sukiennice">Sukiennice</a></i>) in <a href="/wiki/Main_Market_Square,_Krak%C3%B3w" class="mw-redirect" title="Main Market Square, Kraków">Main Market Square</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Built from its earliest nucleus outward, and having escaped much of the destruction endured by Poland during the 20th-century wars, Kraków's many architectural monuments can typically be seen in historical order by walking from the city centre out, towards its later districts. Kraków is one of the few medieval towns in Poland that does not have a historic <a href="/wiki/Ratusz" title="Ratusz">Ratusz</a> <a href="/wiki/Town_hall" class="mw-redirect" title="Town hall">town hall</a> in its <a href="/wiki/Main_Market_Square,_Krak%C3%B3w" class="mw-redirect" title="Main Market Square, Kraków">Main Square</a>, because it has not survived the <a href="/wiki/Partitions_of_Poland" title="Partitions of Poland">Partitions of Poland</a>.</p>
